Phase I Variables (polity-based)[edit]

General variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Lottie Field ♥

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

♠ UTM zone ♣ 49 M ♥

♠ Original name ♣ Medang Kingdom ♥

♠ Alternative names ♣ Sailendra Kingdom; Mataram Kingdom ♥ Mataram Kingdom is a name used to refer to the period in which the kingdom's capital was located in Central Java, between 732 and 918. This name cannot be used in reference to the later period, when the capital shifts to East Java. It must also be distinguished from the much later Islamic polity, the Mataram Sultanate.[1]

♠ Peak Date ♣ 850 CE ♥ The period between 732 and 918 is said to be a golden age of Javanese culture - height of temple building projects and thus the flourishing of art and architecture.[2]


Temporal bounds[edit]

♠ Duration ♣ 732-1019 CE ♥ The definitive end of Medang seems to have come with military defeat in 1006.[3] but it seems there was then a period of fragmentation and turmoil before Airlangga consolidated power beginning in 1019.[4] There are two hypotheses regarding Medang. One suggests that the founder of the Sanjaya dynasty was actually founder of the Sailendra dynasty, which was initially Shivaist Hindu, and changed to Mahāyāna Buddhism on the conversion of his son Panangkaran. [5] The other theory is that there were two competing dynasties within the same polity and the Sailendra gradually assumed dominance. [6]

♠ Degree of centralization ♣ nominal ♥ Political centralization hard to achieve, despite the lack of geographical barriers, due to the influence of local elites in eco-regions. The main political actors were the leaders of eco-region water boards who coordinated the planting of rice. "Kingdoms" began to form on the rice plains through alliance structures. [7]

♠ Supra-polity relations ♣ alliance ♥ 732-800 C.E.: alliance Dharaindra (rr. 780-800) ascended to become the Maharajah of Srivijaya. The nature of the Sailendra's close relationship with the neighbouring Srivijaya empire is complex. It seems that in earlier times, the Sailendra family was within the Srivijayan mandala (sphere of influence), and that later the Sailendra's monarch rose to become the head of Srivijaya. It is uncertain as to whether this was due to a military campaign or close alliance.[8] It seems that over the course of the ninth century, however, any alliance broke down, as in 990 Medang launched a campaign against Srivijaya, leading ultimately to the downfall of Medang in 1006.[9]

Supra-cultural relations[edit]

♠ preceding (quasi)polity ♣ Kalingga Kingdom ♥
♠ relationship to preceding (quasi)polity ♣ continuity ♥ Sanjaya, founder of the Medang Kingdom, was great-grandson of the famous Kalingga monarch Queen Shima. ([1])
♠ succeeding (quasi)polity ♣ Kahuripan Kingdom ♥ Airlangga managed to reunite central and eastern Java after its disintegration into several petty kingdoms following the destruction of the Medang capital in 1006. [10]
♠ Supracultural entity ♣ ♥ No supracultural entity as such, but definitely interaction with other cultures, not least trade with Chinese, Indian, and Arab-speaking peoples.[11] Also evidence of cultural exchange with the Philippines - Laguna copperplate inscriptions from around 900 C.E. discovered in Manila suggests that officials of the Medang Kingdom had connections in regions as far away as the Philippines.[12]
♠ scale of supra-cultural interaction ♣ ♥ km squared.

♠ Capital ♣ Mataram; Mamrati; Poh Pitu; Tamwlang; Watu Galuh; Wwatan ♥ The phrase "Mdaŋ i Bhûmi Matarâm" found in inscriptions means "Medang in the land of Mataram", which means the kingdom name is Medang with its capital in Mataram. [13] The capital was in the mid-ninth century moved to another site in Central Java, Mamrati, and then again around half a century later to Poh Pitu. [14] When Medang moved to East Java around 929, the capital was placed firstly at Tamwlang for a very short time, and then moved to Watu Galuh. [15] It finally moved to Wwatan. [16]


♠ Language ♣ Sanskrit; Old Javanese ♥ Śailendra — Sanskrit for ‘Lord of the Mountain" ([2]). The translations of Hindu epic Ramayana and Mahabharata into old Javanese language took place during the era of the Medang and Kediri Kingdoms. ([3])

♠ Linguistic family ♣ Indo-European; Austronesian ♥ Sanskrit = Indo-European, Javanese = Austronesian ([4])


Map[edit]

[17]


General Description[edit]

The Medang, or Mataram, Kingdom, is the first well-attested Hindu-Buddhist kingdom in central Java. In 732 CE, in an inscription written in Sanskrit, a local ruler called Sanjaya made the 'first definite claim to kingship' known from the region.[18] Over the next few centuries, his successors would extend their influence over areas suitable for irrigated rice agriculture in upland central Java. The Medang Kingdom is famous for its large-scale sacred construction projects, such as the Buddhist pilgrimage site of Borobudur (a series of ascending stone terraces decorated with stone reliefs and topped with stupas) and Prambanan, a Hindu temple complex.[19]
A long-standing debate about dynastic succession in late 1st-millennium CE central Java is still unresolved,[20] showing that there are still many gaps in our understanding of the kingdom. Some scholars hold that there were two competing dynasties, the Buddhist Sailendras and the Shaivite descendants of King Sanjaya, while others believe that the rulers mentioned in inscriptions were two branches of the same family. In the early 10th century CE, the centre of royal power shifted from the high volcanic plains of central Java to the east of the island, signalling the increased importance of maritime trade to the polity.[21]

Population and political organization

King Sanjaya and his successors drew their legitimacy from Indic religious concepts, sometimes devoting themselves to Hindu deities (especially Shiva) and sometimes favouring Buddhist practices.[22][23] They gathered courtly entourages about them and drew their wealth from the taxation of rice and other goods and the use of corvée labour.[24] Local lords, known as rakrayan, and temple foundations could be granted the right to collect taxes from the population within a given parcel of land: these sima tax-grants were a useful way for the Medang kings to reward and ensure loyalty.[25][26]
Secure population estimates for the Medang Kingdom are lacking, but in the early 11th century CE, the East Javanese settlements of Cane, Patakan and Baru each had populations of over 1000 people.[27] The demographer Peter McDonald believes the population of the island as a whole in the 14th century could have been as high as five million.[28] Evidence from Javanese inscriptions suggests that by this time, the population levels of East Javanese states had been rising ‘fairly consistently’ since the early 10th century.[29]

Social Scale[edit]

♠ Polity territory ♣ [100,000-150,000] ♥ km2

Based in Java. Majority of area of Indonesia as a whole at this time covered by another polity, Srivijaya. [5]

♠ Polity Population ♣ [250,000-350,000] ♥ People.

Estimates for Indonesia (less West New Guinea): 2.8m in 700 CE, 3.0m in 800 CE, 3.4m in 900 CE, 3.75m in 1000 CE.[30]

Majority of area of Indonesia at this time covered by Srivijaya.[6] Total area of Indonesia (less West New Guinea) 1,500,000 km2.[31] Medang had about 10% of this area. On basis of area we could estimate for Medang: 280,000 in 700 CE, 300,000 in 800 CE, 340,000 in 900 CE, 375,000 in 1000 CE.

♠ Population of the largest settlement ♣ 1,000 ♥ Inhabitants. The settlements of Cane, Patakan, and Baru, lying just to the south of Surabaya, each appear to have supported populations exceeding a thousand persons by the early eleventh century. [32]

♠ Largest communication distance ♣ [400-500] ♥ in kilometers. This could be a somewhat exaggerated idea, but the Arab writer Abu Zaid wrote that 500 km was the distance of the uninterrupted and regular succession of villages in the Brantas region. [33] ET: Brantas river is 525 km long

♠ Religious levels ♣ [1-2] ♥ levels.

The Sailendra court attracted Buddhist scholars from afar and was a major international centre of Buddhist pilgrimage and learning. [36]

Three major temple complexes - Borobudur (Buddhist, built around 825), Prambanan (Hindu - Shivaist, built around 850), and Dieng (Hindu - Shivaist, completed between the mid-seventh century and the end of the eighth century). Many smaller temples were in addition built by regional coalitions, and each local constituent contributed parts of the temple. [37]


♠ Military levels ♣ [4-5] ♥ levels.

We can infer the presence of a well-organized military. There is evidence for armour[38] and noble cavalry[39] which suggest specialization, and a military campaign was launched against Srivijaya.[40] Elephants were used in warfare[41] and their riders were called maliman.[42]

Law[edit]

♠ Formal legal code ♣ inferred present ♥ Airlangga was said to be the first to codify Javanese law in the period after Medang, although there are written legal documents available from the ninth century. [60] Written legal documents strongly implies there was a formal law process.

♠ Judges ♣ ♥ unknown. written legal documents available from the ninth century. [61] -- who wrote these documents? specialist judges and/or lawyers?

♠ Courts ♣ inferred present ♥ Borobudur reliefs depict the stepped roof type pendopo which once sheltered the institutions of ancient Javanese kingdoms, such as law courts, clergy, palaces, and for public appearances of the king and his ministers. [62]

♠ Professional Lawyers ♣ ♥ unknown. written legal documents available from the ninth century. [63] -- who wrote these documents? specialist judges and/or lawyers?

♠ markets ♣ present ♥ Relationship between village producers and international traders mediated by four layers of merchants and markets: rice, salt, beans, and dyestuffs were taken by the producers to the farmers' market; merchants bought the produce and passed it to intermediary wholesalers; then passed on to merchants on the coast who delivered it to ports; then delivered to international merchants. [66]

Military Technologies[edit]

Military use of Metals[edit]

♠ Copper ♣ present ♥ Island South East Asia: 'Bronze and iron metallurgy appear to have arrived together, perhaps after 300 BC’. [100]
♠ Bronze ♣ present ♥ “Bronze metallurgy was practiced in at least Southern Vietnam, the islands surrounding the Sulu and Sulawesi seas, West Malaysia, South Sumatra, and especially Java and Bali." [101] Island South East Asia: 'Bronze and iron metallurgy appear to have arrived together, perhaps after 300 BC’. [102]
♠ Iron ♣ present ♥ “Iron tools, best evidenced in Southern Vietnam, West Malaysia, and Java, were attached to handles (presum- ably wooden) via tangs, sockets, or shaft holes."[103] Island South East Asia: 'Bronze and iron metallurgy appear to have arrived together, perhaps after 300 BC’. [104]
♠ Steel ♣ present ♥ Historical records show "good quality Indian steel" was reaching Ethiopia in 200 BCE[105] - did they also export across the Bay of Bengal? Island South East Asia: 'Bronze and iron metallurgy appear to have arrived together, perhaps after 300 BC’. [106]

Projectiles[edit]

♠ Slings ♣ inferred absent ♥ Old Mataram was a 'highly Indianized culture' until it was replaced by an East Javanese one "that increasingly promoted various elements of the island's older indigenous traditions."[108] Indian military terms surviving in Javanese: "war, weapon, sword, lance, armour, shield, helmet, banner, battle, siege, fortress, soldier, officer, enemy, spy, etc."[109] In southern India at this time (Rashtrakuta dynasty) military technology included "the sword, the trident or spear, the javelin, the battleaxe, the shield, etc."[110]; while their predecessors had "swords, shields, spears, clubs, lances, bows and arrows etc."[111]
♠ Self bow ♣ present ♥ Borobudur and Prambanan temples contain murals showing the weaponry of early times - swords, bows and arrows, spears, shields, armour, clubs, knives, halberds. The Plaosan temple group 3 miles from Prambanan depicts stone carved gate guards armed with clubs and swords.[112]

♠ Other ♣ present ♥ Bulls - military experts with bulls were called pakarapan. [144]

Armor[edit]

♠ Wood, bark, etc ♣ inferred present ♥ Based on the fact that the Borobudur reliefs depict armour but do not specify which kinds.[145] Old Mataram was a 'highly Indianized culture' until it was replaced by an East Javanese one "that increasingly promoted various elements of the island's older indigenous traditions."[146] Indian military terms surviving in Javanese include 'armour, shield, helmet'.[147]
♠ Leather, cloth ♣ inferred present ♥ Based on the fact that the Borobudur reliefs depict armour but do not specify which kinds - these were the types used later.[148] Old Mataram was a 'highly Indianized culture' until it was replaced by an East Javanese one "that increasingly promoted various elements of the island's older indigenous traditions."[149] Indian military terms surviving in Javanese include 'armour, shield, helmet'.[150]
♠ Shields ♣ present ♥ Borobudur and Prambanan temples contain murals showing the weaponry of early times - swords, bows and arrows, spears, shields, armour, clubs, knives, halberds. The Plaosan temple group 3 miles from Prambanan depicts stone carved gate guards armed with clubs and swords.[151] Old Mataram was a 'highly Indianized culture' until it was replaced by an East Javanese one "that increasingly promoted various elements of the island's older indigenous traditions."[152] Indian military terms surviving in Javanese include 'armour, shield, helmet'.[153] In southern India at this time the Rashtrakuta dynasty used the shield.[154] Their Chalukya predecessors also had shields.[155]
♠ Helmets ♣ inferred present ♥ Old Mataram was a 'highly Indianized culture' until it was replaced by an East Javanese one "that increasingly promoted various elements of the island's older indigenous traditions."[156] Indian military terms surviving in Javanese include 'armour, shield, helmet'.[157]
♠ Breastplates ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Old Mataram was a 'highly Indianized culture' until it was replaced by an East Javanese one "that increasingly promoted various elements of the island's older indigenous traditions."[158] Indian military terms surviving in Javanese include 'armour, shield, helmet'.[159]

Fortifications[edit]

♠ Settlements in a defensive position ♣ present ♥ Convenience and pressures to reduce social tensions appear to have over-ridden considerations of defence in the location of housing from the early tenth century. [172] Ratu Boko - a palace compound converted into a hilltop fortress with defensive structures. [173]
♠ Wooden palisades ♣ present ♥ 'The people make fortifications of wood...' [174] Indian military terms surviving in Javanese include 'fortress' and 'siege'.[175]
♠ Earth ramparts ♣ present ♥ Reference suggested a code of 'present'[176] but no description was provided to explain why. Indian military terms surviving in Javanese include 'fortress' and 'siege'.[177] "'In this country they have made the city walls of piled-up bricks, the wall has double gates and watch-towers,' wrote a Chinese voyager who went to Java fourteen centuries ago."[178]
♠ Ditch ♣ inferred present ♥ Indian military terms surviving in Javanese include 'fortress' and 'siege'.[179] Ratu Boko had a dry moat as a defensive structure [180]
♠ Moat ♣ inferred present ♥ Ratu Boko had a dry moat as a defensive structure [181]
♠ Stone walls (non-mortared) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Ratu Boko had stone walls as defensive structure. [182] Borobudur stone laid without mortar - this was a temple. ([16])
♠ Stone walls (mortared) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Indian military terms surviving in Javanese include 'fortress' and 'siege'.[183]

General Characteristics of Warfare[edit]

Types of Warfare[edit]

♠ Inter-polity wars ♣ present ♥

Dharaindra brought Malay peninsular under Sailendra control.[197]

♠ Internal wars ♣ present ♥

"a war of succession between his younger brother Balputra and his son-in-law Rakay Pikatan. This war of succession must have occurred between 824 and 842."[198]

♠ Intra-elite conflicts ♣ present ♥

"a war of succession between his younger brother Balputra and his son-in-law Rakay Pikatan. This war of succession must have occurred between 824 and 842."[199]

Boechari suggests that a Shivaist warlord took the capital of Mataram.[200]

There were also conflicts between Srivijaya and Medang.[201]

♠ Popular uprisings ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

Did the Hindu-Buddhist conflicts have any popular participation?

♠ Military revolts ♣ suspected unknown ♥ No data yet found in the literature.

♠ Separatist rebellions ♣ present ♥ Prince Jayavarman revolts and claims independence for Mekong delta in Cambodia.[202]

Old Javanese versus Old Khmer (Austronesian versus Austroasiatic).

♠ Settlement destruction ♣ inferred present ♥ According to Wolters (2002) there was an ongoing rivalry, raids and war between the Javanese Medang kingdom and the Sumatra Srivijayan kingdoms over the late tenth and early eleventh centuries during which settlement destruction and massacres may have occurred but there is no definitive confirmation in the sources.[208] Coedes (1965) postulated that "Javanese aggression around 990 probably resulted in a counteroffensive on the part of the Sumatran kingdom. There are good reasons for attributing the Sumatran expedition of 1016-17, the death of the Javanese king, and the destruction of his residence ultimately to this Javanese aggression."[209]

Ritual variables[edit]

A collective ritual is a ritual in which two or more people participate. With the official cult we refer to the set of collective ritual practices that are most closely associated with legitimation of the power structure (including elites, if any).

♠ RA ♣ Lottie Field; Enrico Cioni ♥ LF coded an earlier, pre-expert version of this section.

♠ Expert ♣ ♥

Largest scale collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ temple founding ceremony ♥ "The crowd was rather small, but they represented a broad spectrum of Javanese society. The most exalted were hereditary noblemen. There were several elders from nearby villages, a sculptor from the distant Dieng Plateau (who had probably been invited to the lowlands of what is now the province of Central Java to render professional services), and at least eighteen children. All had gathered to take part in a ceremony sponsored by a local noble, who was present with his wife and three sons. The occasion was the founding of a new temple.//Such gatherings were relatively common in ancient Java. [...]//These ceremonies normally followed a standard format, and that of December 27, 902 C.E., did not deviate from it. The ritual did not take long to complete. First, the noble sponsoring the building of the temple, who is referred to in the account by his tide, Rakai Panggumulan, distributed presents to all the people who had come. The distribution of gifts was an important symbolic act; ancient Indonesians nobility spent much of their energy endeavoring to acquire precious items that they could then redistribute to loyal followers as tokens of spiritual merit. The scribe recorded the noble's bounty in detail. Next, all participants decorated themselves with flowers and paint; we can only try to imagine the colors and patterns they would have drawn upon their bodies. A sacred stone was set in the center of the space where the temple would be built. Offerings were then laid upon it. The people next sat in a circle around the stone; a priest intoned curses against anyone who would dare disturb the peace of their village. With that, the brief ceremony was concluded.//Thereupon began a celebration that probably took longer than the ritual itself. The nobles, elders, sculptor, and others, many of whom were probably commoners from the village, indulged in feasting enlivened by supplies of palm wine, probably drunk from bamboo cups. The repast was succeeded by music and dancing, possibly of a solemn nature; but the Javanese sense of humor was perhaps already as well developed as it is today, for as usual there was also a performance by a buffoon. No doubt he made mild fun of the nobles in attendance. The musicians and clown were entertaining, and they were rewarded for their efforts. [...]" [210]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Such gatherings were relatively common in ancient Java." [211]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Such gatherings were relatively common in ancient Java." [212]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Such gatherings were relatively common in ancient Java." [213]
♠ Duration ♣ [7-8] ♥ hours. This is a guess provided by an expert, John Miksic. [214]
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ unknown ♥ Numbers for this could not be provided by expert (John Miksic) [215]
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ "The crowd was rather small, but they represented a broad spectrum of Javanese society. The most exalted were hereditary noblemen. There were several elders from nearby villages, a sculptor from the distant Dieng Plateau (who had probably been invited to the lowlands of what is now the province of Central Java to render professional services), and at least eighteen children." [216]

No doubt he made mild fun of the nobles in attendance. The musicians and clown were entertaining, and they were rewarded for their efforts. [...]" [218]

The repast was succeeded by music and dancing, possibly of a solemn nature; but the Javanese sense of humor was perhaps already as well developed as it is today, for as usual there was also a performance by a buffoon. No doubt he made mild fun of the nobles in attendance. The musicians and clown were entertaining, and they were rewarded for their efforts. [...]" [256]

Most frequent collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ puja ♥ From the sixth century CE onwards, the puja becomes the main form of Hindu worship, and it involves making offerings (water, unguents, sandalwood paste, incense, food, honey, valuable objects, dairy products) to an image of the deity, and bathing it, as well as chanting, and sharing small quantities of food with other participants [286].
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ daily ♥ Four times daily. Temple puja takes place at sunrise, noon, sunset, and midnight [287].
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ daily ♥ Four times daily. Temple puja takes place at sunrise, noon, sunset, and midnight [288].
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ not applicable ♥ Four times daily. Temple puja takes place at sunrise, noon, sunset, and midnight [289].
♠ Duration ♣ unknown ♥ hour. It is likely this detail cannot be known due to a lack of documentation [290].
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ unknown ♥ It is likely this detail cannot be known due to a lack of documentation [291].
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ elites ♥ Priests at Hindu temples normally belong to the Brahmin caste [292].
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ unknown ♥ It is likely this detail cannot be known due to a lack of documentation [293].
♠ Inclusiveness (audience) ♣ not applicable ♥ Not specified by sources--expert input needed.

Most euphoric collective ritual of the official cult[edit]

♠ Name ♣ temple founding ceremony ♥ "The crowd was rather small, but they represented a broad spectrum of Javanese society. The most exalted were hereditary noblemen. There were several elders from nearby villages, a sculptor from the distant Dieng Plateau (who had probably been invited to the lowlands of what is now the province of Central Java to render professional services), and at least eighteen children. All had gathered to take part in a ceremony sponsored by a local noble, who was present with his wife and three sons. The occasion was the founding of a new temple.//Such gatherings were relatively common in ancient Java. [...]//These ceremonies normally followed a standard format, and that of December 27, 902 C.E., did not deviate from it. The ritual did not take long to complete. First, the noble sponsoring the building of the temple, who is referred to in the account by his tide, Rakai Panggumulan, distributed presents to all the people who had come. The distribution of gifts was an important symbolic act; ancient Indonesians nobility spent much of their energy endeavoring to acquire precious items that they could then redistribute to loyal followers as tokens of spiritual merit. The scribe recorded the noble's bounty in detail. Next, all participants decorated themselves with flowers and paint; we can only try to imagine the colors and patterns they would have drawn upon their bodies. A sacred stone was set in the center of the space where the temple would be built. Offerings were then laid upon it. The people next sat in a circle around the stone; a priest intoned curses against anyone who would dare disturb the peace of their village. With that, the brief ceremony was concluded.//Thereupon began a celebration that probably took longer than the ritual itself. The nobles, elders, sculptor, and others, many of whom were probably commoners from the village, indulged in feasting enlivened by supplies of palm wine, probably drunk from bamboo cups. The repast was succeeded by music and dancing, possibly of a solemn nature; but the Javanese sense of humor was perhaps already as well developed as it is today, for as usual there was also a performance by a buffoon. No doubt he made mild fun of the nobles in attendance. The musicians and clown were entertaining, and they were rewarded for their efforts. [...]" [325]
♠ Frequency for the ritual specialist ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Such gatherings were relatively common in ancient Java." [326]
♠ Frequency per participant ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Such gatherings were relatively common in ancient Java." [327]
♠ Frequency for the audience ♣ suspected unknown ♥ "Such gatherings were relatively common in ancient Java." [328]
♠ Duration ♣ [7-8] ♥ hours. This is a guess provided by an expert, John Miksic. [329]
♠ Typical size of participating group ♣ unknown ♥ Numbers for this could not be provided by expert (John Miksic) [330]
♠ Inclusiveness (ritual specialist) ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Inclusiveness (participants) ♣ whole polity ♥ "The crowd was rather small, but they represented a broad spectrum of Javanese society. The most exalted were hereditary noblemen. There were several elders from nearby villages, a sculptor from the distant Dieng Plateau (who had probably been invited to the lowlands of what is now the province of Central Java to render professional services), and at least eighteen children." [331]

Phase II Variables (polity-based)[edit]

Institutional Variables[edit]

♠ RA ♣ Jenny Reddish; Lottie Field ♥


♠ Expert ♣ ♥

♠ Editor ♣ Daniel Mullins ♥

Limits on Power of the Chief Executive[edit]

Legal (formal) limits[edit]

♠ Executive power is separate or independent from military ♣ absent ♥ Characterizations of kings in some inscriptions do not support a separation between the military and royal power. One Buddhist king (which Sundberg identifies with Panangkaran of the mid-8th century) was described as the 'Killer of Haughty Enemies'.[364] King Balitung (early 10th century) 'extended his polity by conquering the independent east Java rakrayan territory of the Kanjuruhan'.[365]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from legislative ♣ absent ♥ I have found no reference to any kind of independent legislature in the sources. Pigeaud asserts that 'In all periods of Javanese history rulers issued orders and rules pertaining to special aspects of social life, in the first place the social organization of their own Courts and residences, the Royal revenue, and agrarian administration'.[366] As well as charters conferring certain rights and obligations (for instance, tax-free sima status), Old Javanese epigraphy includes 'royal edicts and proclamations', which 'generally begin with the word wruhé or ājñā, Sanskrit for "order, command".'[367]
♠ Executive power is separate or independent from judiciary ♣ absent ♥ 'While in a theoretical sense the king's only duty was to maintain the world order - to protect his realm, promote its prosperity, and facilitate passage to the realm of the ancestors - in practice the monarch's duties sometimes involved the application of customary law regarding land and labor. In an inscription from central Java dated 860, for example, state administrators were asked to intervene in a local dispute when the village elders and representative of a temple could not resolve the method of repaying a considerable debt owed to the local community.'[368] The first king of Medang, Sanjaya, stressed in his Canggal inscription of 732 that he was 'honored by learned men as a scholar in the subtleties of the sastras', which Hall describes as 'Indian legal texts that focus on the maintenance of harmony among the social order'.[369] This implies that the application of laws was considered an key function of kingship in 8th-century central Java.

Legal (formal) freedoms[edit]

♠ Right to petition executive ♣ present ♥ In an article on local and state-level officials in the Medang period, De Casparis writes: 'it is likely that kings regularly visited parts of their realm ... During such tours kings not only showed themelves to the people in areas far away from the kěraton, but also could come into contact with some members of the population ... This gave, however, an occasion to members of the population to present petitions to the king'.[370]​ We could also consider the inscription of Salingsingan (880 CE), which records iron and copper smiths (called 'subalterns' by Zakharov) being summoned to the 'waiting room of the king' over a tax dispute.[371] Zakharov comments that they 'lost the case', using this as an example of the might of the Javanese state,[372] but it does at least indicate that commoners could obtain a hearing in the king's 'waiting room' rather than being arbitrarily punished.
♠ Critique of executive by government ♣ [absent; present] ♥ Writing about the late Medang period, Kinney refers a 'Javanese-style council of military commanders and Hindu and Buddhist priests' being instituted in Bali, implying that this council had previously been part of the Javanese government.[373] Perhaps such a body could criticize and advise the king, but I have not found evidence that this was a formal right.
♠ Critique of executive by non-government ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no reference in the sources to a formal right of any non-governmental, non-religious group to criticize the king.

Power distributed[edit]

♠ Sanction of executive by government ♣ inferred absent ♥ Writing about the late Medang period, Kinney refers a 'Javanese-style council of military commanders and Hindu and Buddhist priests' being instituted in Bali, implying that this council had previously been part of the Javanese government.[374] However, I have not been able to find any account of the rights and duties of this body, so it is unclear whether members had the formal right to to impose sanctions. It seems unlikely given that the inscription of Salingsingan (880 CE) reports some iron and copper smiths as saying 'that nobody could offer opposition to royal command'.[375]
♠ Sanction of executive by non-government ♣ inferred absent ♥ In the inscription of Salingsingan (880 CE), the iron and copper smiths summoned to the king's presence over a tax dispute are reported as saying 'that nobody could offer opposition to royal command and that they should have submitted to Sang Pangaruhan' (a tax collector).[376] Though these inscriptions of course reflect how the monarchs wished to be seen, we could interpret this as an indication that their actions were not subject to sanctions from agents outside the government. Imposing sanctions (such as a fine) would surely qualify as offering 'opposition to royal command'. More generally, Zakharov does not see Medang Java as characterized by 'mutual consent between the rulers and ruled',[377] commenting that the state 'possessed enough power to act independently and arbitrarily on its subjects'.[378]
♠ Constraint on executive by government ♣ inferred absent ♥ Writing about the late Medang period, Kinney refers a 'Javanese-style council of military commanders and Hindu and Buddhist priests' being instituted in Bali, implying that this council had previously been part of the Javanese government.[379] However, I have not been able to find any account of the rights and duties of this body, so it is unclear whether members had the formal right to constrain the monarch's actions. It seems unlikely given that the inscription of Salingsingan (880 CE) reports some iron and copper smiths as saying 'that nobody could offer opposition to royal command'.[380]
♠ Constraint on executive by non-government ♣ inferred absent ♥ In the inscription of Salingsingan (880 CE), the iron and copper smiths summoned to the king's presence over a tax dispute are reported as saying 'that nobody could offer opposition to royal command and that they should have submitted to Sang Pangaruhan' (a tax collector).[381] Though these inscriptions of course reflect how the monarchs wished to be seen, we could interpret this as an indication that royal commands were - at least theoretically - not subject to any formal constraints (veto, overturning decisions etc.) imposed by agents outside the government. More generally, Zakharov does not see Medang Java as characterized by 'mutual consent between the rulers and ruled',[382] commenting that the state 'possessed enough power to act independently and arbitrarily on its subjects'.[383]
♠ Impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no mention in the sources of any legal mechanism for removing the monarch from power.

Customary (informal) checks on executive[edit]

♠ Informal critique of executive ♣ inferred present ♥ Writing about the late Medang period, Kinney refers a 'Javanese-style council of military commanders and Hindu and Buddhist priests' being instituted in Bali, implying that this council had previously been part of the Javanese government.[384] It seems likely that such a body could criticize and advise the king, at least informally.
♠ Informal sanction of executive ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have not found any evidence that any governmental, religious or non-governmental body could impose sanctions on the executive.
♠ Informal constraint on executive ♣ present ♥ Hall argues that 'eighth-century Javanese monarchs such as the Sailendras were important as initiators of a networked cultural integration in central Java. Their power was limited, however. Because they were economic equals to their subordinate rakrayan [regional leaders], and because the developing central Java wet-rice economy was a local and regional one that required minimal centralized economic or administrative leadership by a higher authority, leading rakrayan had few opportunities to dominate except through their ritualized sovereignty and personal power'.[385] Even in the later Medang period, we have evidence of informal constraints on the king's power. Javanese monarchs in the 9th to 13th centuries CE 'did not normally have local land rights, and even their tax rights were subject to social restraint'.[386] Wisseman Christie states that 'The ruler had no power to remove tax rights from his fellow rakais' (lords with tax rights over groups of villages).[387]
♠ Informal impeachment ♣ inferred absent ♥ According to J. G. de Casparis, the 9th-century Medang king Pikatan 'left the throne willingly and became a hermit' (though the Wanua Tengah III inscription seems to suggest that on the contrary, he died on the throne).[388] However, as I have not come across any suggestion that he was pressured into this decision, and as the historical fact of the abdication is itself disputed, I think we do not have enough evidence to code present for informal impeachment.

Limits on executive power imposed by religious agents[edit]

♠ Critique of executive by religious agents ♣ inferred present ♥ At least early on in the Medang kingdom, there is evidence of the kings taking guidance from religious agents: 'In 778, Maharaja Panangkaran founded, at the request of his spiritual teachers, a sanctuary dedicated to the Buddhist goddess Tārā.'[389] This strong influence may imply that spiritual teachers could also express disapproval of the rulers' actions.
♠ Sanction of executive by religious agents ♣ [absent; present] ♥ I have not found evidence that e.g. court brahmins or Buddhist monks could impose formal or informal sanctions on the Medang kings, but it is possible that they wielded sufficient power to be able to withhold co-operation if the king's decisions displeased them.
♠ Constraint on executive by religious agents ♣ inferred present ♥ We could infer that because the Medang kings derived so much of their legitimacy from ritual sources, they would have relied on the support of various religious agents to maintain power. 'Theirs was a "ritual sovereignty" marked by the temple complexes and the patronage of monks and priests steeped in Indian learning who endowed the king with sacred powers and reinforced an aura of divine majesty.'[390]
♠ Constraint on executive by religious institutions ♣ inferred present ♥ Supomo thinks that 'For the elevation of a native [Javanese] chief to the level of ksatriya the Brahmanic rite of vrātyastoma must have been performed by Brahmans. Accordingly, in areas where there was no established Brahman the chief either had to import some from outside - offering them gifts of land and other inducements - or even create them himself from amongst ambitious local groups'.[391] Here we see a need for the would-be ruler to secure the co-operation of priests through gifts and 'other inducements' - it is the institution of the Hindu royal investiture ritual (proposed by Supomo) that acts as a constraint here. This discussion is relevant for the early Medang period (Supomo sees both Sanjaya and his successor Panangkaran as local ratu chiefs elevated to Hindu ritual kingship through these rites), but I think we can also posit a degree of constraint on the later Medang rulers by religious institutions. Reid discusses the relationship of kings in the 'charter era' of 900-1300 CE to Buddhist and Hindu religious specialists and concepts: 'Popular Buddhism remained a key mobilizing factor, but kings were increasingly able to channel it for their own accumulation of sacred power. And for such kings, even while patronizing the Buddhist pilgrimage centers, the hierarchies of Hinduism were indispensable. They required a corps of Brahmins to conduct the rituals of enthronement and legitimation as godlike devaraja or "king of the gods," elevated far above others by the accumulation of supernatural power.'[392] There is a strong sense here that the kings relied on the support of holy men and sacred institutions.
♠ Impeachment by religious agents ♣ inferred absent ♥ According to J. G. de Casparis, the 9th-century Medang king Pikatan 'left the throne willingly and became a hermit' (though the Wanua Tengah III inscription seems to suggest that on the contrary, he died on the throne).[393] However, as I have not come across any suggestion that he was pressured into this decision by religious agents, and as the historical fact of the abdication is itself disputed, I think we do not have enough evidence to code present for impeachment by religious agents.
♠ Subordination of the chief executive to a religious agent ♣ inferred present ♥ A Medang king considered himself 'a titisan dewa - a "droplet distilled from the essence of God" - whose function was to maintain contact between the kingdom on the microcosmic scale and the universe or macrocosm.'[394] This seems to suggest that the kings would have acknowledged the superior power of the gods, deriving their own legitimacy from claims to link the kingdom to the wider universe.
♠ Motivation by belief in supernatural agent ♣ present ♥ Kulke describes religiously motivated state spending on public ceremonies in the early Southeast Asian kingdoms: 'The importance of the personal belief of the rulers and their priests and their magical capability is known to us already from the earliest inscriptional and literary evidence. And on special occasions there were always grand ceremonies, for example, seasonal festivities, potlatch-like "Verdienstfeste" or Vedic rituals'.[395] More specifically for the Medang Kingdom, there are 'donative inscriptions' belonging to the royal family (as well as to local rakrayan) from the temple complex at Candi Plaosan, showing financial support from the monarchy for temple-building.[396][397]

Mechanisms of Power Transfer (from one head of state to the next)[edit]

♠ Customary mechanism ♣ absent ♥ The Wanua Tengah III inscription 'makes clear that the succession was not always from father to son' in the Medang period.[398] Speaking more generally, Heidhues comments that 'Much of the instability of early Southeast Asian realms resulted from internal factors. Important was the lack of fixed rules for royal succession. The title of crown prince was little known, and where it did exist the position immediately made its holder a rival not only to the king himself but to any number of other potential successors. These were numerous because any male closely related to the king - son, brother, uncle, cousin - might have a claim to succeed him. Since most rulers had a number of wives and concubines, their relatives were legion. A woman might also succeed, either in her own name or that of her son or husband, or she might bring in her own family as competitors to the royal line. The female line could be as prestigious as the male. Huge palace complexes might harbour hosts of scheming relatives; poisoning, murder and treachery were by no means unknown at the centre of power'.[399] Oliver Wolters has also argued (in Heidhues' paraphrasing) that 'in the earliest Southeast Asian states power was based not on descent from an established lineage but on individual spiritual qualities'.[400]
♠ Legal mechanism ♣ absent ♥ The Wanua Tengah III inscription 'makes clear that the succession was not always from father to son' in the Medang period.[401] Speaking more generally, Heidhues comments that 'Much of the instability of early Southeast Asian realms resulted from internal factors. Important was the lack of fixed rules for royal succession. The title of crown prince was little known, and where it did exist the position immediately made its holder a rival not only to the king himself but to any number of other potential successors. These were numerous because any male closely related to the king - son, brother, uncle, cousin - might have a claim to succeed him. Since most rulers had a number of wives and concubines, their relatives were legion. A woman might also succeed, either in her own name or that of her son or husband, or she might bring in her own family as competitors to the royal line. The female line could be as prestigious as the male. Huge palace complexes might harbour hosts of scheming relatives; poisoning, murder and treachery were by no means unknown at the centre of power'.[402] Oliver Wolters has also argued (in Heidhues' paraphrasing) that 'in the earliest Southeast Asian states power was based not on descent from an established lineage but on individual spiritual qualities'.[403]
♠ Number of power transitions ♣ 19 ♥ 0. Sanjaya (or Rahyangta i Hara,[404] r. ca. 715-50 CE or 732-60 CE).[405][406] 1. Panangkaran (or Panamkarana,[407] r. 746-84).[408] May possibly be the Sailendra monarch Sanggrāmadhanamjaya.[409] 2. Panaraban (in the Wanua Tengah III inscription) / Panunggalan (in the Mantyasih I inscription) (r. 784-803).[410] May possibly be the Sailendra monarch Dharmmatunggadeva.[411] 3. Warak Dyah Manara (Wanua Tengah) / Warak (Mantyasih) (r. 803-27).[412] May possibly be the Sailendra monarch Samaratungga.[413] 4. Dyah Gula (only in Wanua Tengah, r. 827-29).[414] 5. Garung (r. 829-47).[415] 6. Pikatan Dyah Saladu (Wanua Tengah) / Pikatan (Mantyasih).[416] Identified with 'Jatiningrat' by J. G. De Casparis,[417] r. 847-55.[418] 7. Kayuwangi Dyah Lokapala (Wanua Tengah) / Kayuwangi (Mantyasih) (r. 855-85).[419] 8. Dyah Tagwas (only in Wanua Tengah, r. 885).[420] 9. Panumwangan Dyah Dewendra (only in Wanua Tengah, r. 885-87).[421] 10. Gurunwangi Dyah Bhadra (only in Wanua Tengah, r. 887).[422] The Wanua Tengah III inscription says that he fled from the palace in 887, leaving the country 'leaderless' until 894.[423] 11. Wungkal Humalang Dyah Jbang (Wanua Tengah) / Watuhumalang (Mantyasih) (r. 894-98).[424] 12. Watukura Dyah Balitung (Wanua Tengah) / Watukura (Mantyasih)[425] (r. ca. 907-13).[426] He seems to have conquered the 'independent east Java' but kept central Java as his base.[427] 13. Daksha (r. ca. 913-919).[428] 14. Tulodong (r. 919-21).[429] 15. Wawa (r. 927-28).[430] It may have been he who moved the Medang 'capital' to the east.[431] 16. Sindok (r. ca. 929-48).[432] Sindok 'established a permanent royal kraton in eastern Java and began in earnest the integration of central and eastern Java into a single polity'.[433] 17. Isanatungavijaya[434] (r. ca. 948-9??). 18. Makutavamsavardhana (r. ca. 9??-990).[435] 19. Dharmawangsa Tegu (r. c. 990-1016).[436][437][438] He was the last Medang king. 'The 1041 Calcutta Stone inscription reported that a "great catastrophe" took place in 1016, when Airlangga's predecessor on the throne, Dharmawangsa Teguh, was killed along with his kraton elite by an enemy (Srivijaya), who destroyed the kraton'.[439] NB: Over the past century, historians have held a wide range of opinions about who ruled in central and eastern Java during this period, and recent sources still show no sign of consensus. One theory, whose most famous exponent was probably J. G. De Casparis, posits two dynasties competing for control: the 'indigenous' Shivaist-Hindu Sanjaya and the Mahayana Buddhist Śailendra, 'of foreign origin'.[440] This view has been challenged, particularly by Indonesian scholars who argue for a 'sole lineage', and J. W. Christie has suggested that the Śailendras were merely a branch of the 'Mataram dynasty'.[441] In 1983, the Wanua Tengah III inscription was discovered, featuring a 'more complete list of kings'.[442] Before this date, 'scholars had to make do with the list of [nine] kings in the Mantyasih I inscription of 907', but the Wanua Tengah III inscription includes more kings and even accounts for a period from 887 to 894 in which 'the country was leaderless (anayaka)'.[443] However, there is still no agreement on what this inscription represents: Kusen and Jan Wisseman Christie believe the 'Sailendra' monarchs appear in Wanua Tengah III under different names, while Jordaan maintains that they are not mentioned in this list of Javanese kings 'precisely because of their foreign descent'.[444] To construct a king list for the earlier Medang period, I have followed Jordaan's (2003) table comparing the Wanua Tengah and the Mantyasih inscriptions, including Wisseman Christie's tentative correlations with Sailendra kings.[445] Some of the dates are not secure: different publications give different figures. For the kings after Balitung, I mainly use Kenneth Hall's 2011 account[446] and George Coedès' older work The Indianized States of South-East Asia.[447]
♠ Number of contested transitions ♣ 5 ♥ We have very limited evidence about the lives and reigns of the Medang kings, but there is evidence that at least five transitions were contested. I suspect that given the lack of a legal or widely accepted mechanism for the transfer of power, the majority of transitions would count as 'contested' if we had more information. 1. Sanjaya (r. ca. 715-50 CE or 732-60 CE).[448][449] He was a rakrayan (regional chief) who 'established his legitimacy on the sacred ground of the Dieng Plateau' in Central Java. His reign 'was a critical moment in the development of a central Javanese polity'.[450] The Javanese monarchy was in a nascent phase in the 8th century and Sanjaya had to subdue 'neighbouring raja' to gain power.[451] His Canggal inscription of 732 CE proclaims: 'Then there came forth (one) who was rich in good qualities ... who had his feet high above other kings of good family who were standing on the ground'. I think we can view his 'accession' as contested.[452] 2. Panangkaran (or Panamkarana,[453] r. 746-84). 'Kusen speculates that Rake Panangkaran may have referred to himself as "ornament of the Śailendra family", and that he was the founder of this "dynasty"'.[454] Might his accession thus represent a seizure of power by a rival dynasty? Sundberg believes that Panangkaran 'was the Buddhist king reputed as the "Killer of Haughty Enemies"',[455] and Hall says that 'By the mid-eighth century the Sailendras had replaced Sanjaya's line as the dominant rakrayan among central Java's local rulers.'[456] 3. Jatiningrat/Pikatan (r. 847-55).[457] Coedès claimed that Pikatan warred with his brother-in-law Balaputra and in 856 forced him to flee to Srivijaya, Sumatra.[458] Hall's account of this transition is somewhat similar and reflects the traditional opposition of Buddhist Sailendra to Hindu Sanjaya monarchs: 'In the late ninth century, central Java-based Hindu kings defeated the Buddhist Sailendras and proclaimed their sovereignty over what they called the Mataram state'.[459] However, later scholars such as Zakharov have questioned this interpretation - he is unsure that 'Balaputra' even existed.[460] 4. Panumwangan Dyah Dewendra (r. 885-87).[461] We know from the Wanua Tengah III inscription that his predecessor, Dyah Tagwas, was deposed by a coup d'etat, so we can assume that Dewendra's accession would have been contested.[462] 5. Gurunwangi Dyah Bhadra (r. 887).[463] We know from the Wanua Tengah III inscription that his predecessor, Panumwangan Dyah Dewendra, was deposed by a coup d'etat, so we can assume that Bhadra's accession would have been contested.[464]
♠ Number of successful overturns ♣ 5 ♥ We have very limited evidence about the lives and reigns of the Medang kings, but there is evidence that in at least five transitions, one ruler successfully wrested power from another. I suspect that given the lack of a legal or widely accepted mechanism for the transfer of power, the majority of transitions would count as 'successful overturns' if we had more information. 1. Sanjaya (r. ca. 715-50 CE or 732-60 CE).[465][466] He was a rakrayan (regional chief) who 'established his legitimacy on the sacred ground of the Dieng Plateau' in Central Java. His reign 'was a critical moment in the development of a central Javanese polity'.[467] The Javanese monarchy was in a nascent phase in the 8th century and Sanjaya had to subdue 'neighbouring raja' to gain power.[468] His Canggal inscription of 732 CE proclaims: 'Then there came forth (one) who was rich in good qualities ... who had his feet high above other kings of good family who were standing on the ground'. I think we can view his 'accession' as a successful overturn of the power of these 'other kings'.[469] 2. Panangkaran (or Panamkarana,[470] r. 746-84). 'Kusen speculates that Rake Panangkaran may have referred to himself as "ornament of the Śailendra family", and that he was the founder of this "dynasty"'.[471] Might his accession thus represent a seizure of power from Sanjaya's line by a rival dynasty? Sundberg believes that Panangkaran 'was the Buddhist king reputed as the "Killer of Haughty Enemies"',[472] and Hall says that 'By the mid-eighth century the Sailendras had replaced Sanjaya's line as the dominant rakrayan among central Java's local rulers.'[473] 3. Jatiningrat/Pikatan (r. 847-55).[474] Coedès claimed that Pikatan warred with his brother-in-law Balaputra and in 856 forced him to flee to Srivijaya, Sumatra.[475] Hall's account of this transition is somewhat similar and reflects the traditional opposition of Buddhist Sailendra to Hindu Sanjaya monarchs: 'In the late ninth century, central Java-based Hindu kings defeated the Buddhist Sailendras and proclaimed their sovereignty over what they called the Mataram state'.[476] However, later scholars such as Zakharov have questioned this interpretation - he is unsure that 'Balaputra' even existed.[477] 4. Panumwangan Dyah Dewendra (r. 885-87).[478] We know from the Wanua Tengah III inscription that his predecessor, Dyah Tagwas, was deposed by a coup d'etat, so we can assume that Dewendra's accession represented an overturn of power.[479] 5. Gurunwangi Dyah Bhadra (r. 887).[480] We know from the Wanua Tengah III inscription that his predecessor, Panumwangan Dyah Dewendra, was deposed by a coup d'etat, so we can assume that Bhadra's accession represented an overturn of power.[481]

Central Bureaucracy[edit]

♠ State-level administrators ♣ present ♥ Reed describes the 'personal staffs' assembled by the rulers of emergent Southeast Asian kingdoms: 'the early South-East Asian rulers began to fashion the first indigenous polities by collecting around themselves groups of Brahmanical advisers in order to sanctify their sovereignty, to organize a governmental infrastructure and to supervise a contingent of loyal soldiers...'.[482] De Casparis' has analysed the relations between central and local government in Java from 875 to 950 CE, and lists 'royal officials' as one of the 'formal channels' of contact.[483] Some of these officials served at court, but some resided 'in the villages without apparently being integral parts of the village communities'.[484] As there is no reason to suppose that state-level administrators were absent after 950 CE, and as they were present in the later polities of Kahuripan and Kediri, I have coded present for the remainder of the Medang period.

♠ Prestige of state-level bureaucracy ♣ equal ♥ ​De Casparis makes a loose distinction between 'royal officials' and 'landed nobility' in this period. Royal officials included court officials such as the rakryan mapatih ('prime minister or grand vizir') as well as lower officials associated with the central government but resident in villages as 'outsiders'.[485]​ In de Casparis' schema, the landed nobility are those 'holding titles such as rakai, rakryan (rakarayan), or pamegat'. They could extract labour and other forms of revenue from certain groups of villages known as watak.[486] However, there seems to be some overlap: the above-mentioned rakryan mapatih had a direct relationship with the central government but could also draw wealth from a watak. It seems that both groups included very high-ranking officials as well as lower ones, and in de Casparis' description, neither one seems definitively of higher status than the other.[487]

Authority over state-level administrators

♠ Direct ♣ inferred present ♥ If we agree with Schrieke's mid-century model of the 'administrative structure of the precolonial Javanese kingdom', this would entail accepting that the ruler had direct authority over state-level administrators.[488] Anderson comments that Schrieke's description 'admirably fits Max Weber's model of the patrimonial state'. 'According to this model, the central government is essentially an extension of the ruler's personal household and staff. Officials are granted their positions, and the perquisites that go with them, as personal favors of the ruler, and they may be dismissed or degraded at his whim':[489] Hall says that 'By the tenth and eleventh centuries, royal command "emanated from the maharāja's shoes" through expansive networks of individuals with state and local titles of authority',[490] though his power may have been mediated through these 'networks', clearly the king could issue direct orders to his administrators.
♠ Indirect ♣ inferred absent ♥ If we agree with Schrieke's mid-century model of the 'administrative structure of the precolonial Javanese kingdom', this would entail accepting that the ruler had direct authority over state-level administrators.[491] Anderson comments that Schrieke's description 'admirably fits Max Weber's model of the patrimonial state'. 'According to this model, the central government is essentially an extension of the ruler's personal household and staff. Officials are granted their positions, and the perquisites that go with them, as personal favors of the ruler, and they may be dismissed or degraded at his whim':[492] Hall says that 'By the tenth and eleventh centuries, royal command "emanated from the maharāja's shoes" through expansive networks of individuals with state and local titles of authority',[493] though his power may have been mediated through these 'networks', clearly the king could issue direct orders to his administrators.
♠ Diffused ♣ inferred absent ♥ If we agree with Schrieke's mid-century model of the 'administrative structure of the precolonial Javanese kingdom', this would entail accepting that the ruler had direct authority over state-level administrators.[494] Anderson comments that Schrieke's description 'admirably fits Max Weber's model of the patrimonial state'. 'According to this model, the central government is essentially an extension of the ruler's personal household and staff. Officials are granted their positions, and the perquisites that go with them, as personal favors of the ruler, and they may be dismissed or degraded at his whim':[495] Hall says that 'By the tenth and eleventh centuries, royal command "emanated from the maharāja's shoes" through expansive networks of individuals with state and local titles of authority',[496] though his power may have been mediated through these 'networks', clearly the king could issue direct orders to his administrators.
♠ Mixed ♣ inferred absent ♥ If we agree with Schrieke's mid-century model of the 'administrative structure of the precolonial Javanese kingdom', this would entail accepting that the ruler had direct authority over state-level administrators.[497] Anderson comments that Schrieke's description 'admirably fits Max Weber's model of the patrimonial state'. 'According to this model, the central government is essentially an extension of the ruler's personal household and staff. Officials are granted their positions, and the perquisites that go with them, as personal favors of the ruler, and they may be dismissed or degraded at his whim':[498] Hall says that 'By the tenth and eleventh centuries, royal command "emanated from the maharāja's shoes" through expansive networks of individuals with state and local titles of authority',[499] though his power may have been mediated through these 'networks', clearly the king could issue direct orders to his administrators.
♠ Governmental control ♣ inferred absent ♥ According to Schrieke's mid-century model of the 'administrative structure of the precolonial Javanese kingdom', 'the central government is essentially an extension of the ruler's personal household and staff. Officials are granted their positions, and the perquisites that go with them, as personal favors of the ruler, and they may be dismissed or degraded at his whim'.[500] There doesn't seem to be a clear distinction between state-level administrators and members of government.
♠ Non-governmental oversight ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have not found any indication in the sources that state officials were subject to oversight from any non-governmental body.

Source of Support of state-level officials

♠ State salary ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Governed population ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Land ♣ present ♥ Hermann Kulke has positioned himself in opposition to the 'conventional school' of Southeast Asian historians: he thinks they have too readily applied 'more or less all Weberian criteria of a modern State' to early Southeast Asian states.[501] From this standpoint, he argues that 'the court and its officers in all these [early Southeast Asian] States lived mainly from their own landed property or from benefices with an usufruct which might have been limited in time or heredity ... the court of the Early Kingdom depended mainly on the resources of its direct hinterland'.[502] However, he does not provide more detailed analysis of this for the early Javanese kingdoms. In Schrieke's model of the 'administrative structure of the precolonial Javanese kingdom', discussed by Anderson, 'Payment of officials is essentially in the form of benefices allotted by the ruler for the period of tenure of each particular office'.[503] To follow the Encyclopædia Britannica's definition of a 'benefice', this would have entailed a royal lease of land to support the administrator for the period of office.[504]
♠ None ♣ absent ♥ Kulke argues that 'the court and its officers in all these [early Southeast Asian] States lived mainly from their own landed property or from benefices with an usufruct which might have been limited in time or heredity ... the court of the Early Kingdom depended mainly on the resources of its direct hinterland'.[505] However, he does not provide more detailed analysis of this for the early Javanese kingdoms. Pathologies

♠ Corruption ♣ suspected unknown ♥ The Dinaya Stone of Kañjuruha, dating to 760 CE, shows the king threatening divine punishment for corrupt officials: 'At the end of the record the king stressed that if his relatives and ministers "[had] a revolting attitude towards the king's gifts" (i.e. would like to appropriate them for their personal benefit), they would go to hell. If, on the other hand, they saw fit to "augment the gifts" and performed "meritorious acts like the making of gifts, performances of sacred rites and the study (of the sacred lore), - may they protect the kingdom just like the (present) king"'.[508] The inclusion of this threat shows that the king was concerned about the problem of officials appropriating 'the king's gifts' for themselves rather than redistributing them for the benefit of the prosperity of the realm, but it is not possible to estimate from this information how widespread corruption was in reality.
♠ Bribery ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Abuse of power ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Professionalization of state-level bureaucracy ♣ inferred absent ♥[509] The wording of the Dinaya Stone of Kañjuruha, dating to 760 CE, implies that the king's 'relatives and ministers' were expected to perform non-administrative duties such as 'performances of sacred rites and the study (of the sacred lore)', supposedly for the good of the kingdom.[510]

♠ modal number of years state-level bureaucrats spend in service ♣ suspected unknown ♥ We lack documents that could be used to estimate the modal number of years state- or local-level officials spent in service.

♠ Promotion of state-level officials to other positions ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Frequency of promotion of state-level officials to other positions ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Promotion from lower ranks ♣ present ♥ Van Naerssen notes that after the Raka of Kanuruhan received a sima grant from King Daksa in 915 CE, he was mentioned in subsequent royal charters, 'gradually rising on the list of members of the government'.[511] In Schrieke's model of the 'administrative structure of the precolonial Javanese kingdom', discussed by Anderson, the central government consists of both the ruler's extended family and 'the ministeriales, high-ranking officials of common origin who have risen to power on the basis of their administrative capacities and personal loyalty to the ruler'.[512]

♠ Frequency of promotion of state-level officials from lower ranks ♣ suspected unknown ♥

Local-level officials (provincial, regional, civic administration)[edit]

JR: I'm hoping that the experts might be able to help us code the empty variables and improve on others here. From the English-language sources I have been able to access, the structure of the state- and local-level administration (and the dividing line between these two groups) is not completely clear to me. It would be useful to know whether Schrieke's 'patrimonial bureaucracy' model for precolonial Java is still considered applicable to this period by contemporary historians and archaeologists. Access to large volumes of inscriptions such as Sarkar's Corpus of the Inscriptions of Java may also help to clarify these issues.[513]

♠ Number of regional administrative units ♣ suspected unknown ♥ Wisseman Christie notes that in the surviving inscriptions from central Java between 850 and 928 CE, 'The names of over four hundred wanua [village communities] appear'. These do not correspond neatly to the '120 or so watek tax-groupings ... mentioned in texts of this short period'.[514] The number of wanua and watek seems likely to have varied considerably over the course of the polity. O. W. Wolters applied his concept of the 'mandala' to describe Javanese geopolitical configurations as late as the Majapahit Kingdom (1292-1518 CE),[515] In this view, the state was fluid, defined by its centre rather than its outer boundaries, which shifted back and forth; in Dellios' words, the mandala state 'could be composed of numerous other tributary polities without undergoing administrative integration'.[516] Wolters said that 'Mandalas would expand and contract in concertina-like fashion'.[517]

♠ Local-level administrators ♣ present ♥ An inscription found at Candi Kalasan ('to the east of Yogyakarta') and dating to 778 CE 'lists a number of state officials, relatives of the king, and local nobility who helped to administer clearly defined districts and villages'.[518] More generally, Hall outlines a 'well-established' administrative hierarchy in the Medang state, including state-level and local-level officials. 'Earlier rulers, including the early monarchs of Srivijaya, ruled bhumi, a sphere of authority or realm, which was a more ambiguous statement of territorial authority than rajya. The earliest dated eighth-century Sailendra inscriptions speak of the rajya/state, ruled by a maharaja who defeated neighbouring raja. The rajya/state was administered by a well-established hierarchy of state ministers, local lords, and superintendents of subordinate areas who linked the central court with intermediary, territorially-defined administrative units and villages.'[519]

Authority over local-level administrators

♠ Direct ♣ absent ♥ De Casparis has analysed evidence from the period between 875 and 950 CE and concluded that at this time 'direct control' of local communities 'by the central government had not yet been elaborated in the form which it would adopt later'.[520] Southworth says that 'under the Sailendras [8th-9th centuries] the state of Mataram was largely decentralized. Although minor surrounding kingdoms were absorbed into the main structures of the state, local village communities, or wanua, remained stable and largely independent from the politics of the court, forming their own village networks for the marketing of agricultural produce'.[521] Speaking generally about early Java, Wisseman Christie comments: 'Javanese rulers of successive states seem to have resorted, like their later counterparts, to tax farming as a means of acquiring income without reforming the very decentralized tax-collecting system'.[522] 'Boechari even concluded that "what remained unchanged during the whole [old-Javanese] period was that there never had been a centralized government. The kingdom was divided into a number of autonomous areas, governed by rakais or rakrayans [who] could act independently from the king"'.[523]
♠ Indirect ♣ absent ♥ Southworth says that 'under the Sailendras [8th-9th centuries] the state of Mataram was largely decentralized. Although minor surrounding kingdoms were absorbed into the main structures of the state, local village communities, or wanua, remained stable and largely independent from the politics of the court, forming their own village networks for the marketing of agricultural produce'.[524] Speaking generally about early Java, Wisseman Christie comments: 'Javanese rulers of successive states seem to have resorted, like their later counterparts, to tax farming as a means of acquiring income without reforming the very decentralized tax-collecting system'.[525] 'Boechari even concluded that "what remained unchanged during the whole [old-Javanese] period was that there never had been a centralized government. The kingdom was divided into a number of autonomous areas, governed by rakais or rakrayans [who] could act independently from the king"'.[526]
♠ Diffused ♣ present ♥ Southworth says that 'under the Sailendras [8th-9th centuries] the state of Mataram was largely decentralized. Although minor surrounding kingdoms were absorbed into the main structures of the state, local village communities, or wanua, remained stable and largely independent from the politics of the court, forming their own village networks for the marketing of agricultural produce'.[527] Speaking generally about early Java, Wisseman Christie comments: 'Javanese rulers of successive states seem to have resorted, like their later counterparts, to tax farming as a means of acquiring income without reforming the very decentralized tax-collecting system'.[528] 'Boechari even concluded that "what remained unchanged during the whole [old-Javanese] period was that there never had been a centralized government. The kingdom was divided into a number of autonomous areas, governed by rakais or rakrayans [who] could act independently from the king"'.[529]

Source of Support of local-level officials

♠ State salary ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Governed population ♣ present ♥ In a discussion of the 'landed nobility' (those with rakai, rakrayan or paměgat titles) in the Medang kingdom, de Casparis says that 'Both those with and those without explicit relationships with the court appear to have had some authority over the villages belonging to the watak which is identical with the name in his title. Their authority over such villages included the right to draw certain revenues from these villages. In addition they could exact certain forms of labour from the villages and probably enjoyed other privileges as well'.[533] The 882 CE copperplate inscription of Ramwi, from the Kedu Plain, records village elders successfully petitioning a local rakarayān (regional lord) to reduce their labour duty.[534]

Legal System[edit]

♠ Multiple legal systems ♣ inferred present ♥ Writing about early states in Java and Cambodia, Hall says that 'courts discouraged all but major disputes from clogging the state's administrative system, and settlements encouraged the continuity of local custom (adat), which the state generally left untouched as long as it was not disruptive to state harmony'.[537]

♠ Courts are supported by the state ♣ [absent; present]: 732-900 CE; present: 900-1019 CE ♥ Zakharov comments that the inscriptions of Jayapattra Diedukman (907 CE) and Jayapattra of Vurudu Kudul (922 CE) show 'a legal system supported by the state'.[538] Jayapattra were legal documents awarded to the winners of lawsuits, and were in use from at least the 10th century onwards.[539]

♠ Criminal Prosecution ♣ inferred present: 732-900 CE; present: 900-1019 CE ♥ The 'legal system of ancient Javanese states was well-developed' and we have evidence of a system of criminal prosecution.[540] Jayapattra were legal documents awarded to the winners of lawsuits, and were in use from at least the 10th century onwards.[541] The 10th-century examples 'record the plaint and defense, the type of evidence used in the trial, as well as the decision of the judges', showing a concern for the rule of law and due process.[542] For instance, the Jayapattra of Vurudu Kidul records a 'case of defamation'.[543] The defendant, Sang Dhanadi, was accused of not belonging to any community, but 'was vindicated completely as all her / his ancestors were worthy people'.[544] I have inferred the presence of criminal prosecution in the Medang kingdom before the 10th century because older inscriptions also provide evidence for the influence of 'Hindu ideals of jurisprudence'.[545] King Sanjaya's Tjanggal (Canggal) Inscription of 732 CE compares his father to the Indian lawgiver Manu, and '[a]nother eighth century inscription, a suddhapattra of 794 A.D., demonstrates that Sanskrit pattra (certificates) were being used in legal transactions by the Javanese, indicating not only an awareness of Hindu concepts but also a willingness to utilize them in local administrative operations'.[546]

Accessibility of the Legal System

♠ ruling elite ♣ present ♥ A representative of the ruling elite, a royal official (nayaka) appeared in the tax dispute case recorded in the Palepangan inscription of 906 CE. The elders (ramanta) of Palepangan complained that this official had 'taxed more lands than the community had'.[547]
♠ non-ruling elite ♣ present ♥ The Palepangan inscription of 906 CE records a dispute over taxation levels between village elders (ramanta) and a royal official. The elders of Palepangan complained that the 'royal official (nayaka)' had 'taxed more lands than the community had',[548] showing that these local elites had access to the legal system and could bring cases on behalf of their communities. 'The elders (rāmanta) asked the rakryan mapatiḥ of Hino [one of the high officials in the state of Matarām, a "prime minister" (Barrett Jones 1984: 99)] to take the case.'[549] Zakharov notes that in these records of taxation disputes, it is only the elders (no '[o]ther commoners') who appear. 'The commoners of Palepangan, perhaps, were interested in tax reductions but it remains uncertain whether the elders acted in cooperation with them or not.'[550]
♠ general population ♣ present ♥ Zakharov comments that 'We know of at least two cases of legal assistance for an individual in ancient Java.' For instance, 'the inscription of Jayapattra Diedukman dated from 907 records the case of the debt of a woman who passed away ... The creditor wanted to recover the debt from her husband but the latter did not know about his wife's debt and, according to Old Javanese law, he should not have been a defendant in this case because the debt concerned his wife's property'.[551]
♠ foreign groups ♣ suspected unknown ♥ JR: It has been difficult to find evidence of how foreigners were treated under Old Javanese law. I have included a question about this for the experts in the document for the Singapore workshop. However, the outcome of the case recorded in the Wurudu Kidul jayapattra of 907 CE suggests that being a member of an established wanua (irrigated rice-farming community) conferred special legal rights - 'Only a member of the community (anak wanua) enjoyed full rights in the kěraton society.'[552][553]
♠ ethnic groups ♣ suspected unknown ♥ It has been difficult to find evidence of how different ethnic groups were treated under Old Javanese law, but the outcome of the case recorded in the Wurudu Kidul jayapattra of 907 CE suggests that being a member of an established wanua (irrigated rice-farming community) conferred special legal rights - 'Only a member of the community (anak wanua) enjoyed full rights in the kěraton society.'[554][555] Writing on precolonial mainland Southeast Asia, Scott notes that '[e]cological niche, because it marks off different subsistence routines, rituals, and material culture, is one distinction around which ethnogenesis can occur'.[556] In an ancient Javanese context, this could have meant separate ethnic identities for rice-cultivating anak wanua and swidden cultivators living at higher altitudes, but I have not found concrete evidence for this.
