In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [787]
♠ bravery ♣ inferred absent ♥ Inferred from two things. First, bravery is never mentioned in the literature as one of the main Hindu virtues. Second, it seems that, in Hinduism, punishment or reward for certain behaviours was believed to occur mainly through reincarnation, not through the action of other humans: "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths. According to this system, at death one would pass on to a life in heaven or hell, depending on the merits of one’s activities in life. Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one’s actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [788]
♠ respect for property ♣ inferred absent ♥ Referring to commentaries on Hindu law between the eighth and fourteenth centuries CE: "The law on theft in the period under review seems to have been already well settled much earlier. Texts of this period exhibit near unanimity in their presentations. [...] Some of the subjects highlighted in these texts are: definition of theft, types of theft, punishments for various types of theft, collaborators, special laws of evidence to prove theft, liability of the state to compensate for theft and so on and so forth."[789] However, it seems that Hindu Law had only a nominal link to Hindu precepts, and does not therefore count as "human enforcement of morality": "The growth of law in India may be likened to the evolution of disciplines like grammar, poetics, etymology, and prosody etc. Early speculations on these started in the samhitās and gained strength as auxiliaries to the Vedas. Soon, however, these sciences freed themselves from sacerdotal concerns and came to be established as independent subjects of enquiry, paying only lip service to the authority of the Vedas. The same may be said to be true of law which was an appendage to sacred literature only in the period of the sūtras. Even earlier than the earliest of the known metrical smṛtis, the artha śāstra tradition had recognised that politics and administration were outside the domain of the sacred." [790] Moreover, it seems that, in Hinduism, punishment or reward for certain behaviours was believed to occur mainly through reincarnation, not through the action of other humans: "In this era, the belief in a single life span was replaced by a system of reincarnation called samsara, the cycle of rebirths. According to this system, at death one would pass on to a life in heaven or hell, depending on the merits of one’s activities in life. Then, after a certain amount of time, the individual would be reborn on earth once again. The mechanism that regulated this system was karman, a word that literally means "action." Every action must have an effect. So, the effects of one’s actions in life determine what one will experience after death, and what kind of life one will have in the next birth. In this way, even if a person does not seem to receive all the rewards or punishments deserved in a particular life, the scales will be balanced in the future." [791]

♠ Moralistic punishment by human religious agents ♣ absent ♥ This variable is meant is to reflect whether or not religious agents had the authority to punish moral transgressions independently of the state. In this polity, religious agents did not have authority to punish anyone [792].

Type of punishment this typically entailed (can be more than one):

♠ physical punishment ♣ absent ♥ Religious agents did not have authority to punish anyone [793].
♠ exile ♣ absent ♥ Religious agents did not have authority to punish anyone [794].
♠ loss/seizure of property or wealth ♣ absent ♥ Religious agents did not have authority to punish anyone [795].
♠ loss of status ♣ absent ♥ Religious agents did not have authority to punish anyone [796].

Extent of punishment (can be more than one):

♠ punishment of rulers ♣ absent ♥ Religious agents did not have authority to punish anyone [797].
♠ punishment of elites ♣ absent ♥ Religious agents did not have authority to punish anyone [798].
♠ punishment of commoners ♣ absent ♥ Religious agents did not have authority to punish anyone [799].

Well-Being[edit]

Economic Well-Being[edit]

State Intervention in Economy

♠ The state regulates banking ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Price controls ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Regulations against hoarding/stockpiling goods ♣ present ♥ The Dinaya Stone of Kañjuruha, dating to 760 CE, shows the king threatening divine punishment for officials who hoarded wealth: 'At the end of the record the king stressed that if his relatives and ministers "[had] a revolting attitude towards the king's gifts" (i.e. would like to appropriate them for their personal benefit), they would go to hell. If, on the other hand, they saw fit to "augment the gifts" and performed "meritorious acts like the making of gifts, performances of sacred rites and the study (of the sacred lore), - may they protect the kingdom just like the (present) king'.[800]
♠ Regulations regarding usury ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Debt-relief measures ♣ [absent; present]: 732-800 CE; present: 800-1019 CE ♥ 'Several surviving original texts [from 9th- to 12th-century Java and Bali] do record the clearance of existing debts. In these cases the people concerned are of high social status, and the sums of money considerable. The debts in question, moreover, are long-standing and often inherited, suggesting that indebtedness was a common condition even for the elite'.[801] Those in debt-bondage could also find sanctuary by fleeing to the tax-grant (sima) communities where they were 'protected from seizure by their masters'.[802]
♠ Pensions ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Standardization of weights/measures ♣ [absent; present]: 732-843 CE; present: 843-1019 CE ♥ 'In the corpus of surviving original - rather than later reissued - Old Javanese language inscriptions, the earliest mention of a system of weights and values is dated 843 A.D., and it comes from the state of Mataram.'[803] By 860 CE, 'a uniform system of monetary weight-values had already been recognized by the court' and was used for expressing the value of 'taxes and tithes, the value of labour and services, interest and repayment schedules on debt, and transactions involving commodities ranging from land and buildings down to domestic food purchases'.[804] The Mananjung inscription of 928 CE provides evidence for the involvement of the east Javan kings in regulating trade, including a royal instruction on 'the use of standards and approved weights and measures at warehouses and rice granaries'.[805][806]
♠ Guarantee stability of coin value ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Public markets ♣ present ♥ Already by the 8th and 9th centuries in central Java, there was a 'hierarchical marketing network' based at the simplest level on periodic village markets known as pken or pkan.[807] '[T]he pken markets provided the forum for the watak's [village cluster's] external commercial contacts, the place where "foreign" merchants were allowed to trade, and thus facilitated the flow of local production upward to supralocal market centers as well as receiving in return the downward flow of foreign goods for local consumption from the supralocal markets.[808]

Contracts

♠ State enforcement ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Non-state enforcement ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Corporate Agency ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ State contracting ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Limited Liability ♣ suspected unknown ♥

Patronage

♠ within-class formal patronage ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ between-class formal patronage ♣ [absent; present]: 732-800 CE; present: 800-1019 CE ♥ Helen Creese has described how the 'Indic courts' of pre-Islamic Java functioned as 'major centres of patronage of the arts', including of court poets who composed kakawin (literary epics) between the 9th and 15th centuries.[809] Creese comments that 'For their material well-being kakawin poets needed more than an insubstantial relationship with a deity or muse, no matter how spiritually uplifting, and were dependent on royal patronage',[810] so I think we can view this as a formal relationship.
♠ within-class informal patronage ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ between-class informal patronage ♣ suspected unknown ♥

Public Goods[edit]

♠ Famine relief ♣ inferred absent ♥ Speaking generally about wet rice-cultivating (sawah) areas in early southeast Asia, Hall says that during famines, the population would resort to eating taro, yams and sago palms, which were normally disdained: 'Rice might be affected by periodic disease and plagues; mice plagues are shown in Borobudur (Java) and Angkor (Cambodia) temple relief. But fish (from rivers, tanks, or sawah fields) and coconut (when properly maintained) were virtually free of pests and diseases. Rice was eaten by preference to other grains or other kinds of starches. Reliance upon other staples was looked down upon except during rice famines when sawah cultivators could normally turn to tubers (taro, which grew in sawah areas) and yams (which were gathered from nearby forests or were grown in the rain-fed fields) as well as to sago palms, which were another stable source of starch.'[811] Takács and Cline comment that 'Early Asian rice cultivators also supplemented their diet by networking with highland hunters and gatherers, both to reduce the highlanders' inclination to raid their villages and to exchange their diverse agricultural produce for forest products (such as woods, bamboo and lacquer) and meat'.[812] I have not come across any evidence of the deliberate provision of famine relief by any state, religious or non-state agents, and Wisseman-Christie says 'there is no evidence that [9th-century Javanese villagers] maintained communal granaries'.[813]

♠ provision of famine relief ♣ inferred absent ♥ As far as I can tell, the rice-farming population would simply adopt different subsistence strategies in times of famine, turning to taro, yams and sago and trading more with 'highland hunters and gatherers'.[814] I have not come across evidence for public storehouses of rice or other staples set aside for famine relief.

♠ Food storage ♣ inferred absent ♥ The Mananjung inscription of 928 CE provides evidence for the involvement of the east Javan kings in regulating trade, including a royal instruction on 'the use of standards and approved weights and measures at warehouses and rice granaries'.[815][816] The wealth of the eastern Javanese Medang state (from the early 10th century onwards) 'was ultimately based on their ability to stockpile rice, which they traded for the spices and other rainforest products harvested in the other parts of the eastern archipelago.'[817] However, I have not come across any sources suggesting that these storage facilities functioned as public goods, and Wisseman Christie claims that 'there is no evidence that [9th-century Javanese villages] maintained collective granaries or that they had communally-farmed fields. Nor did they feed non-farming but productive members of the community through the kind of redistributive system identified by Hegel and Marx as the hallmark of the traditional Asian village'.[818] She thinks the professional rice vendors connected to the periodic early Javanese markets may have 'served not only the consumption needs, but also some of the storage and credit needs of the farmers'.[819]

♠ provision of food storage ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Alimentary supplementation ♣ inferred absent ♥ Speaking generally about wet rice-cultivating (sawah) areas in early southeast Asia, Hall says that during famines, the population would resort to eating taro, yams and sago palms, which were normally disdained: 'Rice might be affected by periodic disease and plagues; mice plagues are shown in Borobudur (Java) and Angkor (Cambodia) temple relief. But fish (from rivers, tanks, or sawah fields) and coconut (when properly maintained) were virtually free of pests and diseases. Rice was eaten by preference to other grains or other kinds of starches. Reliance upon other staples was looked down upon except during rice famines when sawah cultivators could normally turn to tubers (taro, which grew in sawah areas) and yams (which were gathered from nearby forests or were grown in the rain-fed fields) as well as to sago palms, which were another stable source of starch.'[820] Takács and Cline comment that 'Early Asian rice cultivators also supplemented their diet by networking with highland hunters and gatherers, both to reduce the highlanders' inclination to raid their villages and to exchange their diverse agricultural produce for forest products (such as woods, bamboo and lacquer) and meat'.[821] I have not come across any evidence of alimentary supplementation as a public good provided by some state, non-state or religious body: as far as I can tell, the rice-farming population had several techniques at their disposal (such as trade with forest hunter gatherers) to supplement their diet.

♠ provision of alimentary supplementation ♣ ♥

♠ Drinking water ♣ inferred absent ♥ Miksic believes that 'the unique problems connected with maintaining healthy drinking water supplies in a tropical environment help to explain why early urbanization in Indonesia ... is relatively rare'.[822] We do not have solid archaeological evidence for urban sites in central or east Java until the 14th-century (Majapahit) site of Trowulan, which features canals and reservoirs that may have served both symbolic and practical functions.[823] If we follow his line of argument, we might infer that the Javanese before the 14th century had no need for public drinking water distribution systems (irrigation is another matter) because they were not clustered in dense settlements; or, conversely, that the early Javanese did not live in dense settlements because the problems of providing clean drinking water in tropical urban settings had not yet been solved. Either way, I think we can infer an absence of systems for providing drinking water to the general population.

♠ provision of drinking water ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Irrigation works ♣ present ♥ Network of drainage systems. [824]

♠ provision of irrigation works ♣ mixed ♥ There is much discussion of irrigation management in the literature. 'Wisseman Christie argues that the irrigation systems in ancient Java were built and maintained by the communities themselves (1992, 2007).'[825] However, Zakharov is doubtful and notes that already by the 5th century in western Java, a ruler called Purnavarman 'undertook certain water management works'.[826] In the early Medang period, irrigation provision seems to have been relatively decentralized - managed at the level of a cluster of villages. Hall says that the Sailendra dynasty developed in central Java as regional leaders of a watak, a regional unit integrating 'village clusters (wanua) participating in a regional irrigation and/or otherwise networked society'.[827] He refers to the 'autonomous watak irrigation systems of central Java' under the Sailendras.[828] From 900 CE onwards, accompanying the eastward shift of the centre of Medang power, the state took an increasingly active role. Several 10th-century inscriptions record 'an east Java-based monarch's involvement in the system's construction or in organizing the continuing direction of local elders or regional political authorities to guarantee that the hydraulic system would be properly maintained or managed "for the benefit of the rakrayan's people." Local village elders were instructed by the rakrayan to see that farmers were fully aware of the significance of regulations affecting the use of this new irrigation system and that they were to abide by established rules applying to water distribution'.[829] An inscription of 905 CE records local rakrayan lords receiving rewards from King Balitung for the construction of an aqueduct.[830] I think this variable is best coded as 'mixed' throughout the period, as we can see the involvement of both state and non-state actors in irrigation.

♠ Health infrastructure ♣ present ♥ Some temples had ritual bathing places known as petirtaan[831] - for instance, the terraced ponds built into a mountain at the Hindu temple of Jolotundo (977 CE).[832] 'These bathing places were usually constructed at the site of a natural spring'.[833] It seems likely that the pools were conceived as having curative properties in addition to their sanctity: Boomgaard has described how in indigenous thought across Southeast Asia, water is 'a healing force'.[834] Later on in the history of Indonesia, '[i]n many instances, bathing a patient in water was the only 'remedy' indigenous healers would apply, as in the case of small-pox, besides incantations and offerings, of course'.[835]

♠ Control of infectious disease ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Care of war invalids/veterans ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no evidence of the public provision of care for war veterans.
♠ Care of orphans ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no evidence of the public provision of care for orphans.
♠ Care of elderly ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no evidence of the public provision of care for the elderly.

♠ State pensions for elites ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no mention of state pension provision in any of the Hindu-Buddhist Javanese kingdoms.

♠ State pensions for non-elites ♣ inferred absent ♥ I have found no mention of state pension provision in any of the Hindu-Buddhist Javanese kingdoms.

Entertainment and Knowledge Well-Being[edit]

♠ Funding for festivals ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Theatres ♣ inferred absent ♥ We know that after the move to east Java, the bestowal of a sima freeholding grant by the king on a certain temple and community was followed by ritual feasting and '[e]ntertainment of various kinds', including '[d]ancing, gambling, cockfights, drama performances by masked players, music and singing, puppet (wayang) performances, and recitals of the Ramayana'.[836] However, I have not found any references to buildings set aside for theatrical performance.
♠ Arenas ♣ ♥
♠ Ritual buildings/spaces ♣ present ♥ This period saw a spate of religious building activity, in which many Buddhist (e.g. Borobudur, Candi Mendut, Candi Kalasan and Candi Sewu, all associated with the Sailendra dynasty)[837] and Hindu temples (e.g. the 9th-century complex at Prambanan)[838][839] appeared.
♠ Other ♣ ♥
♠ accessibility of entertainment buildings ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Full-time entertainment specialists ♣ [absent; present]: 732-800 CE; present: 800-1019 CE ♥ In a discussion of the structure of the Javanese state from the 9th to 13th centuries, Wisseman Christie lists 'magicians ... actors, musicians, and dancers'. One of their sources of support seems to have been tax-collecting rights bestowed by the king.[840]

♠ status of full-time entertainment specialists ♣ suspected unknown ♥

♠ Access to education ♣ all people ♥ De Casparis and Mabbett describe how a 'true alternative society', a religious and scholarly community devoted to 'an archaic form of Brahmanic Hinduism', flourished on the Dieng Plateau 'from early times to at least the eleventh century'. Access to education here may have been limited to the 'higher classes' as it was in the Indian āśramas.[841] However, other forms of education seem to have been available to a wider range of people. Kakawin epic poems, of which our earliest example is a 9th-century version of the Ramayana, were performed before 'a live audience drawn from all classes of society ... In addition, didactic elements in kakawin, particularly those pertaining to proper conduct, or dharma, were aimed at society in general'.[842] Though Creese does not explicitly say that this included women, I think we can assume that it does - she also writes that 'didactic elements in kakawin, particularly those pertaining to proper conduct, or dharma, were aimed at society in general', before speculating on the degree to which 'ordinary Javanese and Balinese men and women actually embraced' the moral code conveyed by the epics.[843] Supomo has described how from at least the early 10th century, Sanskrit literary culture was spreading from the kraton into rural areas: he believes there were public readings of Sanskrit epics as in the Indian harikathas.[844]

♠ Publicly funded or subsidized education ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Schools for children? ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Universities, “proto-universities”, or other higher education ♣ present ♥ 'The Sailendra court attracted Buddhist scholars from afar and was acclaimed internationally as a major center of Buddhist pilgrimage and learning'.[845]
♠ Libraries ♣ [absent; present]: 732-800 CE; inferred present: 800-1019 CE ♥ Creese refers to kakawin literary works being preserved in 'royal libraries' and copied and recopied over centuries.[846] She does not specify when these libraries were first established in Java, but the earliest surviving kakawin is an Old Javanese version of the Ramayana from the 9th century.[847] Sundberg believes that 'Sinhalese ascetic monks' practising an esoteric form of Buddhism were invited to Java and allowed to establish a monastery on the Ratu Baka Plateau in 792 CE because they 'had the extensive library of tantras; they had the mastery of esoteric rituals ...'.[848]
♠ Museums ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Literacy rate ♣ rare ♥ In his book on statelessness in Southeast Asia, Scott argues that 'literacy in premodern societies was, under the best of circumstances, confined to a minuscule portion of the population, almost certainly less than 1 percent ... To assert, in this context, that a whole society or people is literate is incorrect; in all premodern societies the vast majority of the population was illiterate and lived in an oral culture, inflected though it was by texts'.[849]

Biological Well-Being[edit]

Mortality

♠ Life expectancy ♣ suspected unknown ♥ John Miksic told us that although we don't have enough skeletal evidence to draw conclusions on people's health in the ancient Javanese kingdoms, scholars suspect that it was quite good.[850] Because we lack evidence either from skeletal analyses or from any kind of record-keeping on births and deaths, I think we cannot come up with a figure for average life expectancy at this time. The long-term annual rate of population growth in premodern Indonesia is generally believed to have been very low, hardly exceeding 0.1 % per annum up to 1750. According to Anthony Reid "constant low-level warfare" led to high deaths in pre-modern Indonesia - the mobilization of large armies and the taking of large numbers of captives sold into slavery. [851]
♠ Infant mortality ♣ suspected unknown ♥ John Miksic told us that although we don't have enough skeletal evidence to draw conclusions on people's health in the ancient Javanese kingdoms, scholars suspect that it was quite good.[852] Because we lack evidence either from skeletal analyses or from any kind of record-keeping on births and deaths, I think we cannot come up with a figure for infant mortality at this time. David Henley believes the absence of weak and unhealthy adults observed by European travellers suggests high rates of infant mortality as only the strong could survive regular food shortages. [853]

Disease

♠ Endemic disease ♣ {absent; present} ♥ Different sources give varying impressions of prevalence of disease in the ancient Javanese kingdoms. Scott writes that the 'demography and agro-ecology of state space ... makes it more vulnerable to instability in food supply and to illness ... we have ample evidence of crop failures, famines, and cholera outbreaks in precolonial Southeast Asia. Although somewhat speculative, it is likely that the concentration of rice and men in state spaces carried their own substantial risks'.[854] His comments are oriented toward the 'padi states' of precolonial mainland Southeast Asia, but may be considered relevant to the irrigated rice cultivators in Hindu-Buddhist Javanese states. Miksic comments that 'Irrigation can ... be associated with water-borne diseases. A particularly important example is urinary schistosomiasis, which is spread by contact with water where there live snails which form the intermediate host. A study in Madagascar found that urinary schistosomiasis spread rapidly to new areas along with the expansion of an irrigation system ... It is not known whether such problems occurred in ancient Java, but one must remember that the expansion of the irrigation system may well have caused new health problems while eliminating others'.[855] However, Miksic also told us that although we don't have enough skeletal evidence to draw conclusions on people's health in the ancient Javanese kingdoms, scholars suspect that it was quite good.[856] He believes that the rarity and late appearance of cities in Indonesia may have been a way of avoiding the higher disease burden and other 'dangers and inconveniences' associated with urban settlement patterns.[857]
♠ Disease outbreaks ♣ inferred present ♥ Lovric comments that 'Considering Java's geographical position on the trade-route between India and China and the centuries-old two-way traffic, it seems unlikely that the island could have been by-passed by pandemics of the plague until the twentieth century'.[858] Sustained maritime trade between India and Southeast Asia can be dated to the 'later centuries of the pre-Christian era'.[859]
♠ Endemic malnutrition ♣ {absent; present} ♥ Different sources give varying impressions of the diet of the average subject of the ancient Javanese kingdoms. Scott writes that the 'demography and agro-ecology of state space ... makes it more vulnerable to instability in food supply and to illness ... we know that the grain diet of early agrarian societies was nutritionally inferior to the mixed diet it replaced; and finally, we have ample evidence of crop failures, famines, and cholera outbreaks in precolonial Southeast Asia. Although somewhat speculative, it is likely that the concentration of rice and men in state spaces carried their own substantial risks'.[860] His comments are oriented toward the 'padi states' of precolonial mainland Southeast Asia, but may be considered relevant to the irrigated rice cultivators in Hindu-Buddhist Javanese states. On the other hand, Jákl thinks we can infer from the descriptions of food in the kakawin epics that the peasant diet was relatively rich and varied. He comments: 'It may be wrong to extrapolate modern dietary patterns of Javanese peasants, with their heavy reliance on rice and vegetables, back on the conditions of pre-Islamic Java. In relatively sparsely populated premodern Java, with abundant resources and few documented dietary taboos, peasants may have generally enjoyed a much more varied diet then their modern descendants. It seems that predominantly, or purely, vegetarian dietary regimes were a matter of a conscious choice rather than a necessity'.[861]

Physiology

♠ Average height (male) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ John Miksic told us that we don't have enough skeletal evidence to draw conclusions on people's health in the ancient Javanese kingdoms.[862] Reports from early European travelers often emphasised the tall stature and good health of the local population which Reid believes indicates favourable living conditions. [863] Barrow reports that the Javanese in 1793 were "the middle size of Europeans". [864]
♠ Average height (female) ♣ suspected unknown ♥ John Miksic told us that we don't have enough skeletal evidence to draw conclusions on people's health in the ancient Javanese kingdoms.[865] Reports from early European travellers often emphasised the tall stature and good health of the local population which Reid believes indicates favourable living conditions. [866] Barrow reports that the Javanese in 1793 were "the middle size of Europeans". [867]

Prosocial Behavior[edit]

♠ Volunteering ♣ present ♥ Monuments such as Borobudur were constructed by the compulsory but sometimes voluntary labour of artisans and agriculturalists.[868] In addition, the inscription of Wuatan Tija provides some evidence that members of local communities may have engaged in military activity of their own accord: 'During the reign of King Lokapāla Kayuwangi, the community of Wuatan Tija saved a royal child named Bhūmijaya from the rebellious prince of Laṇḍhayan who had already killed another royal child, the prince of Mānak ... Therefore the community was bestowed to Bhūmijaya as a sīma. The salvation of the royal child appeared to have been possible only if this community possessed military might and political will.'[869] However, the details of this military operation are vague: 'we do not know which parts of the community argued for the active position and how this saving of a royal child was organized.'[870]

♠ Charity ♣ present ♥ Inscriptions on the smaller temple structures at Candi Plaosan show that various dignitaries and officials made donations towards the cost of the building.[871] De Casparis and Mabbett conclude from this that 'officials or local chiefs in different parts of the kingdom were asked or ordered to contribute to the royal foundation, thus attesting both their piety and their loyalty.'[872] Although they may well have been ordered to do so, the donations appear to have been framed as 'pious contribution[s]',[873] so could perhaps be categorized as charity rather than a form of tax.

Voluntary Associations

♠ voluntary trade associations ♣ [absent; present] ♥ The kabanyagan ('merchant group') of Galuh is mentioned in the Linkatan Charter of 919 CE, but it is not clear whether this was a voluntary association or a group of hereditary traders.[874]
♠ prestige of voluntary trade associations ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ voluntary community organizations ♣ [absent; present] ♥ By the 9th century CE, Javanese villages featured a governing council: 'the karāmān - whose membership was large, running at times into the hundreds'. However, from Wisseman Christie's description it appears that membership was not entirely voluntary. The karāmān 'appears to have comprised all core or full-membership, adult, married villagers, their status probably dependent upon a combination of land rights and position as head of household'.[875]
♠ prestige of voluntary community organizations ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ voluntary athletic clubs ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ prestige of voluntary athletic clubs organizations ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ other voluntary organizations ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ prestige of other voluntary organizations ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ voluntary religious associations ♣ present ♥ De Casparis and Mabbett describe how a 'true alternative society' of ascetics and monks devoted to 'an archaic form of Brahmanic Hinduism' flourished on the Dieng plateau in central Java 'from early times to at least the eleventh century'.[876] More generally, maritime Southeast Asia was influenced by 'an important feature of the Indian way of life': 'the possibility to opt out of ordinary society to choose a life of contemplation - either as a hermit in the forest or as a member of some religious community'.[877]
♠ prestige of voluntary religious associations ♣ equal ♥ The Brahmanic áśramas found in early Java were probably very prestigious, as they attracted the 'higher classes' and were perceived as places where one could acquire 'wisdom' and teach others.[878] We can infer from religious iconography that ascetic gurus enjoyed very high status in Javanese thought. For instance, the niche usually occupied in South Indian Śiva temples by 'Śiva Dakșiņāmūrti, the "Supreme Guru"' is replaced in Java by a 'representation of the sage Agastya. It may seem strange that a ŗși [an ascetic or court brahmin], however wise and powerful he may have been, should be represented on a par with deities like Durgā and Ganesa, but it is known that Agastya, for whom a temple and an image were made in east Java in 760, enjoyed special veneration in Java. Whichever is the correct interpretation, there is no doubt that the Guru was one of the most popular representations in ancient Java. This may well reflect the particular awe felt for a teacher in Java till recent times'.[879] For this reason, I have coded 'equal': those who joined these communities seem to have been equal in status to the top nobility.

Economy variables (polity-level)[edit]

State Income[edit]

♠ Tribute ♣ present ♥ 'Like the temples, the kings, too, were the recipients of tribute (often rice) from their allies'.[880]

♠ Staple ♣ present ♥ 'Like the temples, the kings, too, were the recipients of tribute (often rice) from their allies'.[881]
♠ Labor ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Currency ♣ suspected unknown ♥

Debt and Indemnities

♠ Polity has obligation to other polity or group ♣ suspected unknown ♥
♠ Polity receives obligatory payment from other polity or group ♣ suspected unknown ♥

Taxation[edit]

♠ Degree of taxation ♣ suspected unknown ♥ one sixth of the rice harvest: 900-1000 CE In his article focusing on the social history of 10th-century Java, de Casparis stated that 'Le roi était puissant: il pouvait lever des impôts, en principe un sixième de la récolte des rizières' (The king was powerful: he could levy taxes, in principal one sixth of the rice harvest).[882] However, in general, 'there are few indications in the Old Javanese charters as to precisely how tax obligations were paid and the channels through which payments went before reaching the paramount ruler', so it seems unlikely that we can come up with a figure for the total tax collected.[883] The tax burden probably increased over time: 'In the highly personalized, early central Java-based polities, the monarch had limited revenue demands, which were consistent with his own inability to make large-scale revenue collections',[884] but in the late 9th century the list of tax collectors in charters 'grows exceedingly long and detailed'.[885]

Taxes are imposed on:

♠ Land ♣ present ♥ One 10th-century sima inscription 'records the annual tax in silver currency paid ... on stated square measures of wet rice field, and adjustments made following complaints from the farmers'.[886]
♠ Property ♣ inferred present ♥ The presence of a sima system of tax exemption for religious sites implies that taxes can be placed on land and property. [887]
♠ People ♣ present ♥ As well as taxes on land, the 10th-century Watukura inscription from east Java stipulates that 'Each household will also offer 1 kupang in gold and 1 kupang-worth of rice at the full moon of the month of Cetra (March-April)'.[888]
♠ Trade ♣ [absent; present]: 732-800 CE; present: 900-1019 CE ♥ In an article focussing on the 9th to 13th centuries in Java, Wissman Christie says: 'Markets and trade are mentioned frequently in sīma charters as important sources of tax revenue'.[889]'The tax provisions of the charters of the ninth century were fairly vague and sweeping. By the tenth century, however, when market trade began to receive greater administrative attention, limits were imposed upon the volume of trade that could be carried on by individual professionals resident in sima villages without being subject to state taxes'.[890] 'Inscriptions report that rulers of east Java's states received specified shares of ... goods supplied regularly by domestic traders, such as spices, ceramics, and cloth of foreign origin.'[891] The Dhinanasrama inscription of the 10th century CE records a Javanese ruler lifting taxes on maritime trade: 'a Javanese ruler removed royal taxes from twenty vessels and the shippers of goods of twelve different classes - a total of 135 vessels were operating from one north coast port, reflecting a sizeable trade'.[892]
♠ Income ♣ [absent; present]: 732-875 CE; present: 875-1019 CE ♥ In an analysis of the titles and roles of central and local government officials in central and eastern Java between 875 and 950 CE, de Casparis writes that many of the administrative staff of local rakai likely 'controlled some particular kinds of revenue, such as taxes on different kinds of specialized labour: mask cutters, potters, etc.'.[893] This sounds like a tax on these artisans' income. I have coded present for the remainder of the Medang period because of the continuity in Javanese institutions after the move to east Java, demonstrated by Krom (though 'Major changes' in administration occurred during Airlangga's reign and in the Kediri Kingdom, after the period of this polity).[894]
♠ Production ♣ present ♥ 'Inscriptions report that rulers of east Java's states received specified shares of local products such as rice and cloth'.[895]

Taxes are imposed by:

♠ executive or ruling elite ♣ present ♥ A portion of taxes always had to go back to the royal court.[896] The presence of a sima system of tax exemption for religious sites implies that taxes are imposed by the ruling elite. [897] Writing on taxation in early 10th-century Java, Wisseman Christie says: 'For sawah (irrigated rice fields), ... the palace appears to have set the tax rate according to a formula based primarily upon the total square measure of the fields that a community had under cultivation. This measurement was carried out by intermediate-level officials who, since they apparently kept a portion of the tax for themselves, had a vested interest in maximizing tax yield.'[898]
♠ local elites ♣ present ♥ Regional landlords (rakai) have authority to collect taxes, but a portion must go to the ruling elite.[899] Hall mentions 'tax collectors and officials' working for 'local rulers or village authorities' in 10th- and 11th-century Java.[900] Wisseman Christie says that 'Agricultural taxes in the early tenth century were still assessed on communities as a whole. The internal distribution of the tax burden was, at least in part, handled by the village council.'[901]
♠ state officials ♣ present ♥ We have evidence from as early as 778 CE - from the Sanskrit inscription of Kalasan - that officials called manilala drawya haji, 'those claiming the king's property', collected taxes from certain villages.[902] They were overseen by 'a trio of royal officials', the 'three honoured ones'.[903] The Palepangan inscription of 906 CE (which records a tax dispute) also shows that state-level officials were responsible for some tax collection. The elders of the Palepangan community complained that the 'royal official (nayaka)' had 'taxed more lands than the community had'.[904] Taxes could also be collected by religious agents on behalf of the monarch, as we know from the inscription of Salingsingan (880 CE). This records the refusal of certain iron and copper smiths to pay taxes to a 'religious authority called Sang Pangaruhan'.[905] During the 'era of east Javanese hegemony', foreign port-based merchants could be 'employed as royal tax collectors in areas near their port of residence'.[906] Hall thinks that the use of foreign tax collectors at this time might indicate political instability and an 'attempt by the Javanese monarch to penetrate local political autonomy'.[907]

Type of taxes paid:

♠ Staple ♣ present ♥ Rice surpluses collected from the land were used to build or maintain religious buildings.[908] 'Central Java states ... expected both taxes in kind and labor service from their subjects. Inscriptions report that rulers of east Java's states received specified shares of local products such as rice and cloth, as well as goods supplied regularly by domestic traders, such as spices, ceramics, and cloth of foreign origin'.[909]
♠ Labor ♣ present ♥ 'The pre-1500 political systems, whether wet-rice or riverine, commonly attempted to draw the resources of their realms - in the form of tribute, talent, men, and goods - to their centers. Central Java states, for instance, expected both taxes in kind and labor service from their subjects'.[910]
♠ Currency ♣ absent: 743-950 CE; present: 950-1019 CE ♥ By 860 CE, 'a uniform system of monetary weight-values had already been recognized by the [Medang] court' and was used for expressing the value of 'taxes and tithes'.[911] But de Casparis argues for the period before 950 CE that 'Although the amounts of taxes on land are always mentioned as sums of money (i.e. weights of precious metal) in the inscriptions, it is unlikely that they should have been paid in this form. Relatively few gold and silver coins have been found in Java, and all those that have been found are of low denominations ... In some cases, higher sums of money may have been paid in the form of rings (simsim) and other ornaments, but this entailed practical difficulties and cannot therefore have been the usual practice. It therefore seems quite likely that the land tax was usually paid in kind in accordance with the value of the gold and silver weights mentioned'.[912] I have coded present for the period after 950 CE because in a paragraph discussing tax and trade in Java from the 10th century onwards, Wisseman Christie states clearly: 'Gold and silver coins were minted in both central and east Java, and they were used by all strata within the society to pay taxes and debts'.[913] One sima charter from the 10th century 'records the annual tax in silver currency paid ... on stated square measures of wet rice field, and adjustments made following complaints from the farmers'.[914]

Wages and Costs[edit]

♠ Wages ♣ suspected unknown ♥ I have not found a figure for the annual salary of a labourer for the Medang, Kahuripan or Kediri Kingdoms, but I suspect that this may be due to lack of access to some key volumes of collected inscriptions. The information may be available in Barrett Jones (1984) or de Casparis (1950, 1956).[915][916] Wisseman Christie comments that 'The contents of the surviving inscriptions of ninth and early tenth century Java suggest that ... the monetary price of commodities and labour ... remained rather fluid for some time after the introduction of coinage',[917] which implies that we may be able to glean some figures from the inscriptions.

♠ staple cost of principal source of carbohydrates ♣ 10 ♥ masa (= 24 grams of gold) for one buffalo, according to the Jurungan Charter of 876 CE.[918] I have not yet found a figure for the cost of rice or other staple carbohydrates, but 'The contents of the surviving inscriptions of ninth and early tenth century Java suggest that ... the monetary price of commodities and labour ... remained rather fluid for some time after the introduction of coinage.'[919]

♠ Top fortune ♣ suspected unknown ♥ I have not found any estimates of the top fortune of any particular individual or family during this period. However, the Wonoboyo Hoard, which dates from the 10th century or earlier and 'appears to comprise the entire contents of the strong room of a branch of the royal family',[920] contains 'approximately 9,000 objects of which 6,336 are gold coins and circa 600 are silver coins'.[921] Hall describes wealthy members of the court elite in the 10th-13th centuries as well as newly rich families. 'Javanese consumerism in the tenth through thirteenth century era included kraton members as well as the socially ambitious.'[922] 'As one historian characterizes this age: "Public grandeur [i.e., the construction of lavish temple complexes] was, for a time, displaced by private luxury"'.[923]

♠ Other wealth distribution ♣ suspected unknown ♥ According to Hall, the distribution of resources was one of the sources from which the regional lords (rakrayan) of central Java drew their power: 'Foundational to a rakrayan or ratu lord's claim to sovereignty was his continuing ability to establish reciprocity relationships and redistribution networks in which material and psychological well-being were perceived to be a consequence of local submission to an overlord's authority.'[924] The Dinaya Stone of Kañjuruha, dating to 760 CE, shows the king threatening divine punishment for officials who hoarded wealth: 'At the end of the record the king stressed that if his relatives and ministers "[had] a revolting attitude towards the king's gifts" (i.e. would like to appropriate them for their personal benefit), they would go to hell. If, on the other hand, they saw fit to "augment the gifts" and performed "meritorious acts like the making of gifts, performances of sacred rites and the study (of the sacred lore), - may they protect the kingdom just like the (present) king'.[925] However, new forms of wealth and power based on commerce seem to have emerged around the turn of the millennium: Hall describes wealthy members of the court elite in the 10th-13th centuries as well as newly rich families. 'Javanese consumerism in the tenth through thirteenth century era included kraton members as well as the socially ambitious.'[926] 'As one historian characterizes this age: "Public grandeur [i.e., the construction of lavish temple complexes] was, for a time, displaced by private luxury"'.[927]
